    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="e5abfaac11604dbe89f6a545685ab508"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e5abfaac11604dbe89f6a545685ab508">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd8cf1b26-2339-43a3-aa7a-932061a0c774'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-4084-1125"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0002-4084-1125</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Department of Chemical Engineering, KU Leuven, Celestijnenlaan 200F, Heverlee, B-3001 Belgium</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:tangbohejin@sues.edu.cn"><span>tangbohejin@sues.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:ruiyc@sues.edu.cn"><span>ruiyc@sues.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:lei.jiang@kuleuven.be"><span>lei.jiang@kuleuven.be</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Jiang/Lei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d140478333" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), as the next-generation high-performance electrochemical energy storage devices, have attracted widespread attention due to their cost-effectiveness and wide geographical distribution of sodium. As a crucial component of the structure of SIBs, the anode material plays a crucial role in determining its electrochemical performance. Significantly, metal phosphide exhibits remarkable application prospects as an anode material for SIBs because of its low redox potential and high theoretical capacity. However, due to volume expansion limitations and other factors, the rate and cycling performance of metal phosphides have gradually declined. To address these challenges, various viable solutions have been explored. In this paper, the recent research progress of metal phosphide materials for SIBs is systematically reviewed, including the synthesis strategy of metal phosphide, the storage mechanism of sodium ions, and the application of metal phosphide in electrochemical aspects. In addition, future challenges and opportunities based on current developments are presented.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310426-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310426-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the vigorous development of the industrial economy, and increasing energy demand, fossil fuels have become the most important energy all over the world, such as coal, oil, and natural gas.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0001_R_d140478325e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - SusMat">1</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0006_R_d140478325e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nature">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The burning of these non-renewable fuels also contributes to the greenhouse effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0007_R_d140478325e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Fuel Process. Technol.">7</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0011_R_d140478325e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Int. J. Energy Econ. Policy">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At present, the global energy conflict has deteriorated sharply, and it is urgent to develop and utilize new energy sources to alleviate the energy crisis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0012_R_d140478325e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0013_R_d140478325e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Electrochim. Acta">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are one of the most desirable energy storage devices due to their excellent characteristics such as high energy density,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0014_R_d140478325e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Jom">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> long cycle life,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0015_R_d140478325e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Phys. Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and high charging efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0016_R_d140478325e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - SusMat">16</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0024_R_d140478325e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Mater. Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the abundance of Li in the earth's crust is only 0.0017&nbsp;wt%, which is far from being able to meet the needs of global resource storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0025_R_d140478325e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small Methods">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the uneven distribution of lithium resources around the globe can cause prices to spike easily. Currently, electrochemical energy storage devices mainly rely on lithium-ion batteries. However, high costs and low resource reserves have inhibited the application of lithium-ion batteries in large-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0026_R_d140478325e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0028_R_d140478325e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the development of an alternative battery technology is imminent.</p>
         
         <p>The energy storage mechanism and design principles of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are similar to those of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0029_R_d140478325e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - SusMat">29</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0035_R_d140478325e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium and lithium belong to the same main group of elements and have similar physical and chemical properties. However, the abundance of sodium in the earth's crust is thousands of times greater than that of lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0036_R_d140478325e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Technol.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to the standard hydrogen electrode (SHE), SIBs exhibit a reoxidation-reduction potential of −2.71&nbsp;V,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0037_R_d140478325e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> surpassing that of the lithium electrode by 0.3&nbsp;V. And the cost of SIBs is relatively low.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0038_R_d140478325e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, many researchers regard SIBs as one of the most powerful alternative energy storage systems to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0039_R_d140478325e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Rev.">39</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0040_R_d140478325e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Same as lithium-ion batteries, SIBs are mainly composed of four parts: cathode, anode, separator, and electrolyte. The working mechanism of the SIBs is referred to as the “rocking chair” mechanism, wherein the sodium potential differs between the materials of the cathode and anode. During the charge and discharge processes, the sodium ions are inserted/extracted between the cathode and anode, thereby achieving energy conversion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0041_R_d140478325e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Storage Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0001">1</a>, during the charge process, sodium ions are extracted from the layers of the cathode and inserted into the layers of the anode after passing through the electrolyte. The process of discharge is reversed, with sodium ions moving from the anode to the cathode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202310426-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/837e3c9d-13da-4f8a-8438-220b46c6d657/smll202310426-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/837e3c9d-13da-4f8a-8438-220b46c6d657/smll202310426-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/21210add-be8a-476e-b072-091cd92687da/smll202310426-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/837e3c9d-13da-4f8a-8438-220b46c6d657/smll202310426-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202310426-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202310426" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Mechanism of sodium-ion batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Electrode materials with excellent performance are the key to ensuring that the battery has excellent multiplication performance, cycle stability, and high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0042_R_d140478325e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Energy Storage">42</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0045_R_d140478325e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ChemistrySelect">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon is one of the most promising anode materials in the field of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0046_R_d140478325e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Prog. Energy">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Its microstructure is characterized by amorphous carbon. In contrast to graphitic carbon, its layer arrangement is disordered, enabling sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0047_R_d140478325e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">47</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0048_R_d140478325e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Energy Lett.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sun et&nbsp;al. used ginkgo biloba leaves as precursors to synthesize hard carbon within a wide pyrolysis temperature range of 600–2500&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0049_R_d140478325e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relationship between the microstructure of the synthesized hard carbon and the storage behavior of sodium ions was investigated. However, hard carbon materials with sodium intercalation mechanisms exhibit low working voltages and are prone to sodium dendrite formation, posing safety concerns.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0050_R_d140478325e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Electrochim. Acta">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the same time, its theoretical specific capacity is also quite limited. In recent years, there has been an increasing number of studies on novel high-performance sodium ion storage materials, including carbon materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0051_R_d140478325e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0052_R_d140478325e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chemosphere">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metals,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0053_R_d140478325e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - ACS Nano">53</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0054_R_d140478325e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Nano">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> alloys,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0055_R_d140478325e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Small">55</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0056_R_d140478325e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0057_R_d140478325e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nat. Commun.">57</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0058_R_d140478325e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nano Energy">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sulfides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0059_R_d140478325e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">59</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0060_R_d140478325e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> phosphorus and metal phosphides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0061_R_d140478325e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Angew. Chem.">61</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0065_R_d140478325e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - ACS Nano">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the above anode candidates, phosphorus-based compounds have attracted much attention and become a research hotspot in the field of electrochemistry due to the abundant storage of phosphorus (P) in recent years. The lower redox potential (0.4&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of P further reduces the risk of sodium dendrite formation during the charge and discharge processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0066_R_d140478325e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Adv. Energy Mater.">66</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0067_R_d140478325e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Energy Storage Mater.">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Phosphorus possesses multiple electron orbitals, thereby exhibiting superior chemical properties. In 2013, Kim et&nbsp;al. and Qian et&nbsp;al. first reported the sodium storage behavior of phosphorus with excellent electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0061_R_d140478325e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Angew. Chem.">61</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0068_R_d140478325e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Adv. Mater.">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Phosphate, as a phosphorus-based material, exhibits significantly reduced practical capacity compared to its theoretical capacity due to the presence of conductivity-limiting defects, consequently resulting in limited cycle life. Recently, a large number of studies have shown that phosphorus combined with germanium (Ge),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0069_R_d140478325e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Energy Storage Mater.">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> tin (Sn),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0070_R_d140478325e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Adv. Mater.">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> copper (Cu),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0071_R_d140478325e658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - J. Mater. Chem. A">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and other metals to form metal phosphide can effectively overcome its inherent defects and improve the reversibility, conductivity, and stability of phosphorus-based anode materials to a certain extent. This is due to some metal phosphides undergoing conversion into pure metal elements during their reaction with Na, thereby reducing the material pulverization caused by volume expansion and simultaneously enhancing the electrode's conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0072_R_d140478325e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Chem. Commun.">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310426-fig-0002">2</a>, the application of metal phosphide in the anode of SIBs still has some disadvantages, such as volume expansion and poor electrical conductivity, causing capacity fading. Many promising methods have been used to solve this problem. Liu et&nbsp;al. prepared homogeneous yolk–shell Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>@C nanospheres by top-down phosphorization pathway using yolk–shell Sn@C nanospheres as precursors. As an anode material for SIBs, it has high reversible capacity and superior multiplicity performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0073_R_d140478325e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Energy Environ. Sci.">73</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fullenwarth et&nbsp;al. reported a promising phosphorus-rich phosphide NiP<sub>3</sub> and found that it has good sodium ion storage behavior. The voltage distribution indicates that it has a relatively low redox potential (≈0.2&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), which is favorable for improving the safety and energy density of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0074_R_d140478325e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - J. Mater. Chem. A">74</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Brehm et&nbsp;al. prepared Cu<sub>3</sub>P/C by the ball milling method and studied its electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0075_R_d140478325e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 75 - J. Power Sources Adv.">75</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the electrolyte is diglyme, the Cu<sub>3</sub>P/C exhibits a capacity of 120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 120 cycles. In 2014, Kim et&nbsp;al. pioneered the study of Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> as an anode material for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0076_R_d140478325e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 76 - Adv. Mater.">76</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results show that the Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> anode still has a reversible capacity of 718 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles of charge and discharge. TEM, X-ray absorption fine structure spectroscopy (EXAFS) and XRD analyses revealed the structural changes and sodium storage mechanism of Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> during the process of sodium insertion/extraction. In the sodium insertion process, the phosphorus in Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> first generates amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P. Subsequently, Sn and Na combine to form NaSn particles. Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> particles are then uniformly dispersed in amorphous Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P to prevent particle agglomeration during charging and discharging. In the process of sodium extraction, Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> is gradually formed, which indicates that the material has favorable cyclic stability and reversibility.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202310426-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/40261825-0cd6-481d-9301-ee7d2d180f78/smll202310426-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/40261825-0cd6-481d-9301-ee7d2d180f78/smll202310426-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce1b7bed-893d-4776-890e-0ff5563d0284/smll202310426-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/40261825-0cd6-481d-9301-ee7d2d180f78/smll202310426-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202310426-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202310426" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Advantages and disadvantages of metal phosphide in SIBs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Currently, metal phosphide as an anode material of SIBs is a hot research topic.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0077_R_d140478325e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 77 - Adv. Energy Mater.">77</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0079_R_d140478325e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 79 - Electrochim. Acta">79</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The latest research advances of metal phosphide as anode is reviewed in this paper. The storage mechanism of sodium, the synthesis methods of metal phosphide, and the improvements made by researchers to enhance the electrochemical performance are introduced. Finally, based on this latest scientific research, we prospect the existing opportunities and challenges. It is hoped that the review of this paper will shed light on the research of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310426-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310426-sec-0020-title">2 The Mechanism of Sodium Storage</h2>
         
         <p>Metal phosphide can improve the conductivity of phosphorus-based anode materials to a certain extent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0080_R_d140478325e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">80</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0081" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0081_R_d140478325e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 81 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">81</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Metal phosphide systems (MP<sub><i>x</i></sub>, M = Sn, Fe, Co, and Ni) exhibit better reversible capacity, cycling stability, and rate capability by enhancing particle conductivity in SIBs. Due to the fact that certain metal phosphides can undergo conversion into pure metallic elements during the sodium insertion process, it effectively mitigates the volume expansion caused by material pulverization while simultaneously enhancing the conductivity of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0072_R_d140478325e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Chem. Commun.">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the production of an intermediate phase (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>M or Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P) also acts as a buffering mechanism to ameliorate volume expansion.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">There are mainly two kinds of sodium storage mechanisms of metal phosphide, including conversion type and conversion-alloy type. The conversion type mainly includes CuP<sub><i>x</i></sub>, FeP<sub><i>x</i></sub>, and NiP<sub><i>x</i></sub>. These metal phosphides generally undergo Na<sup>+</sup> insertion or extraction by conversion reactions. In the process of sodium storage, the metal element is obtained by reduction, which enhances the conductivity of the electrode material. Phosphorus in metal phosphide forms Na<sub>3</sub>P with sodium by electron transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0082_R_d140478325e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - Nano Res.">82</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific reaction process is shown in Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202310426-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202310426-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e15af304-18c3-4a65-82b8-500d6c1b086b/smll202310426-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202310426:smll202310426-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202310426-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mi>x</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mi>x</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo>↔</mo>
                           <mi>x</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\rm M{P}_x + 3{\it x}N{a}^ + + 3{\it x}{e}^ - \leftrightarrow {\it x}N{a}_3P + M\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Among the converted metal phosphide, CuP<sub>2</sub> is taken as an example. After sodium ions are inserted, they react with CuP<sub>2</sub> to form Na<sub>3</sub>P and Cu.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0083" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0083_R_d140478325e884" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 83 - J. Mater. Chem. A">83</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When sodium ions are extracted, Na<sub>3</sub>P and Cu react to form CuP<sub>2</sub>. During the first discharge, the insertion of sodium ions is usually accompanied by the breaking of phosphorus─metal bonds, leading directly to the formation of nanoscale metal nanocrystals and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P phase. At present, it is generally believed that the metallic Cu nanoparticles formed in the intermediate stage provide highly conductive electron channels, facilitating rapid electric charge transfer kinetics. Moreover, the elastic buffering matrix mitigates the volume expansion of P during subsequent sodium ion insertion/extraction processes, thereby enabling rapid electrochemical reactions. Conversion-alloy type mainly includes GeP<sub><i>x</i></sub>, SnP<sub><i>x</i></sub>, ZnP<sub><i>x</i></sub>, and so on. In addition to conversion reactions, these metal phosphides undergo alloying reactions during the sodium ion insertion/extraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0082_R_d140478325e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - Nano Res.">82</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific reaction processes are shown in Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202310426-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202310426-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202310426-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6523530a-3566-43bb-af77-e9dffe852074/smll202310426-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202310426:smll202310426-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202310426-math-0002.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                                 <mi>x</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">y</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                                 <mi>x</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">y</mi>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfenced>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo>↔</mo>
                           <mi>x</mi>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">y</mi>
                           </msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\rm M{P}_{\mathrm{x}} + \left( {3{\it x} + y} \right)N{a}^ + + \left( {3{\it x} + y} \right){e}^ - \leftrightarrow {\it x}N{a}_3P + N{a}_yM\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Converted-alloyed metal phosphides, taking Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> as an example, react with Na<sup>+</sup> during insertion to form Na<sub>3</sub>P and Na<sub>y</sub>Sn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0084" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0084_R_d140478325e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 84 - Nano Lett.">84</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon extraction of sodium ions, Na<sub>3</sub>P and Na<sub>y</sub>Sn undergo a reaction to revert to Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>. And the sodium is extracted and reduced back to the original phase. The cycle reaction is carried out sequentially. This excellent electrochemical performance is attributed to the collaborative nano-storage reaction of Sn and P components in Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> crystal during the initial charge process. Following the amalgamation of Sn and P components, the dispersed Sn nanoparticles serve as electron conduits for the electrochemical activation of the P component. P and Na<sub>3</sub>P as a shielding matrix reduced the concentration of Sn particles and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> during the Na insertion. Thus, the entire anode electrochemical microenvironment remains stable. At present, studies on the mechanism of sodium storage in metal phosphide are still immature, necessitating further investigation and exploration.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310426-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310426-sec-0030-title">3 Synthesis Method</h2>
         
         <p>There are many ways to synthesize metal phosphide. Hydrothermal solvothermal method, high-energy ball milling (HEBM), chemical vapor deposition, and phosphating are emphasized in this section. <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202310426-tbl-0001">1</a> shows the electrochemical properties of anode materials synthesized by these methods for SIBs.</p>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="smll202310426-tbl-0001">
            
            
            
            
            <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  1.
                    </span>Representative synthesis methods and electrochemical performance of various metal phosphides</header>
            
            
            
            <div class="article-table-content-wrapper" tabindex="0">
               <table class="table article-section__table">
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  
                  <thead>
                     
                     <tr>
                        
                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell right-bordered-cell left-aligned">Metal phosphide</th>
                        
                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">Initial specific capacity</th>
                        
                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">Initial Coulomb efficiency [%]</th>
                        
                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">Cycling performance</th>
                        
                        <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">Capacity retention rate [%]</th>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310426-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310426-sec-0040-title">3.1 Hydrothermal Solvothermal Method</h3>
            
            <p>Hydrothermal solvothermal method is regarded as one of the ideal synthesis methods because of its low reaction temperature and simple reaction steps.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0093" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0093_R_d140478325e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 93 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">93</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0094" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0094_R_d140478325e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 94 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">94</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a drawback of this method is the non-uniform morphology of the synthesized nanoparticles. Zhou et&nbsp;al. synthesized the Ni<sub>2</sub>P/CoP@rGO heterostructure with a micro-flower shape by hydrothermal method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0095" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0095_R_d140478325e1402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 95 - J. Power Sources">95</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The flower shape of Ni<sub>2</sub>P/CoP@rGO consists of ultrathin nanosheets with rough surfaces. Ni<sub>2</sub>P/CoP@rGO nanosheets have a large number of pores, which increase the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> transport channel and improve the electrolyte permeability. HRTEM image of the material (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310426-fig-0003">3a</a>) reveals two sets of stripes with spacing distances of 0.22 and 0.28&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the Ni<sub>2</sub>P(111) and CoP(011) crystallographic planes, respectively. This indicates the presence of a Ni<sub>2</sub>P/CoP heterojunction interface. The Ni<sub>2</sub>P/CoP heterostructure can improve reaction kinetics and electron/ion transport rates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0096" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0096_R_d140478325e1429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 96 - ACS Nano">96</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0003">3d</a>, Zheng et&nbsp;al. synthesized α-Ni(OH)<sub>2</sub> on reduced graphene oxide by hydrothermal reaction. Ni<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub>@rGO, 3D-layered Ni<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub> nanospheres, is coated with graphene oxide nanostructures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0085" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0085_R_d140478325e1449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 85 - J. Mater. Sci.">85</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ni<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub> microspheres uniformly embedded in reduced graphene oxide (rGO) nanosheets serve to not only restrict the substantial volume variation in metal phosphide but also mitigate the capacity degradation over successive cycles. Additionally, this integration enhances the electrochemical performance of SIBs, contributing to improved overall efficiency. Because of the unique microstructure and synergistic effect between Ni<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles and rGO nanosheets, Ni<sub>5</sub>P<sub>4</sub>@rGO composites exhibit excellent sodium storage properties. Sun et&nbsp;al. used a solvothermal combined thermal decomposition reaction to uniformly coat nickel phosphide (NiP) nanoparticles in a layered carbon shell (NiP@C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0086" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0086_R_d140478325e1467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 86 - Electrochem. Commun.">86</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the synthesis process of the NiP. The outstanding electrochemical performance of NiP@C is attributed to the effective alleviation of volume expansion in the NiP anode throughout cycling, facilitated by the presence of carbon (C) filling between the NiP particles. This strategic incorporation of carbon serves to effectively mitigate the challenges associated with volume changes during the cycling process, thereby contributing to the overall enhanced performance of the NiP@C system. It can reach a respectable capacity of 385.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. It maintains a capacity of 292&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even after 100 cycles. In addition, the NiP@C electrode exhibits faster kinetics than NiP owing to the better electronic/ionic conductivity of carbon.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202310426-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b9941230-10f8-4e7a-a968-68b3763bb842/smll202310426-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310426-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310426-sec-0050-title">3.2 High Energy Ball Milling</h3>
            
            <p>HEBM uses mechanical energy to induce chemical reactions or to induce changes in the structure and properties of materials to prepare new materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0098" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0098_R_d140478325e1556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 98 - Sustainable Energy Fuels">98</a>, <a href="#smll202310426-bib-0099" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0099_R_d140478325e1556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 99 - J. Energy Chem.">99</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can obviously reduce the activation energy of the reaction, refine grain sizes, greatly increase the powder activity, and improve the particle distribution uniformity, representing an energy-saving and efficient preparation method. Through the HEBM process, smaller particle sizes and larger surface area can be obtained, providing more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, thus achieving higher specific capacity. Liu et&nbsp;al. prepared Zn<sub>0.5</sub>Ge<sub>0.5</sub>P compounds by HEBM.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0097" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0097_R_d140478325e1568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 97 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">97</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the ball milling process, Zn, Ge, and P powders can be directly converted into Zn<sub>0.5</sub>Ge<sub>0.5</sub>P compounds by high energy and high-pressure collision. The synthesis process is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0003">3f</a>. The synthesized Zn<sub>0.5</sub>Ge<sub>0.5</sub>P has a particle size ranging from several tens of nanometers to a few micrometers with excellent electrochemical performance. Kaushik et&nbsp;al. synthesized polycrystalline VP<sub>2</sub> by HEBM.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0087" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0087_R_d140478325e1587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 87 - J. Power Sources">87</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The polycrystalline nature of the material is evident from the bright spots (Debye–Scherrer rings) along the circular path in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0003">3b</a>). The electrochemical performance of SIBs is evaluated using VP<sub>2</sub> crystal material as the anode and ionic liquid as the electrolyte at 90&nbsp;°C. The material demonstrates an initial discharge capacity of 243&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Zhang et&nbsp;al. synthesized a novel Sn/P@G-CNTs composite as a high-performance anode material for SIBs by a low-cost and simple HEBM method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0088" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0088_R_d140478325e1602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 88 - Electrochim. Acta">88</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0003">3c</a>, Sn and P particles are evenly distributed in the G-CNTs matrix. The particle size ranges from 100&nbsp;nm to 10&nbsp;µm. The random distribution of clustered particles results in a loose structure. It is worth noting that the tubular structure of carbon nanotubes can be observed in the SEM image except for some structural damage during the ball milling process. A single carbon nanotube is typically in the range of ≈50–300&nbsp;nm in length. The incorporation of a G-CNTs matrix gives rise to a loosely structured Sn/P@G-CNTs composite. This architectural arrangement plays a crucial role in alleviating the adverse effects of substantial volume changes encountered during the sodium insertion/extraction processes. Consequently, the cyclic stability of the material is significantly enhanced.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310426-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310426-sec-0060-title">3.3 Chemical Vapor Deposition</h3>
            
            <p>Chemical vapor deposition (CVD) is a process that uses gaseous substances to produce solid deposition by chemical and transport reactions on a solid.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0100" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0100_R_d140478325e1620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 100 - Nano Energy">100</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0104" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0104_R_d140478325e1620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 104 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">104</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The focus of this method is to select the appropriate gas source. Qiu et&nbsp;al. synthesized Fe<sub>2</sub>P nanowires with controllable diameter and carbon shell thickness through chemical vapor deposition, employing environmentally friendly solid ion resin as the phosphorus source, while adjusting the resin quantity and holding time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0089" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0089_R_d140478325e1627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 89 - Chem. Eng. J.">89</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis process has a “solid-gas-liquid-solid” mechanism. With the increase in temperature, the resin decomposes into carbon gas (CR) and phosphorus gas (P-R), which act as carbon and phosphorus sources respectively. The gas reacts with the metal foam substrate to form a solid solution. When the inner carbon (C) reaches saturation, it precipitates outside the Fe<sub>2</sub>P nanowires to form a core-shell structure. The synthesis process is illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310426-fig-0004">4a</a>. “Solid” corresponds to resin. “Gas” refers to the decomposition of resin into gas. “Liquid” refers to the formation of a solid solution at high temperatures. And “solid” refers to the final solid state Fe<sub>2</sub>P@C. The resulting Fe<sub>2</sub>P@C can be used directly as an electrode in the SIBs without any additional additives. Due to the advantages of CVD, the core-shell structure of uniformly carbon-coated Fe<sub>2</sub>P nanowires can effectively buffer the volume expansion and fracture of the nanowires, and keep the microstructure of the electrode from collapsing. This effectively improves the cyclic performance of the electrode material. In addition, due to the unique core-shell structure design and the direct growth of the foamed iron base, a highly conductive electron conduction network is formed.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202310426-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca7ffbb2-9a1e-466c-b480-c9619bffa6c6/smll202310426-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca7ffbb2-9a1e-466c-b480-c9619bffa6c6/smll202310426-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d071a987-1bf3-455c-a08a-5b872f329ad6/smll202310426-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca7ffbb2-9a1e-466c-b480-c9619bffa6c6/smll202310426-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202310426-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202310426" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) An illustration of the general synthetic process in schematic form for the Fe<sub>2</sub>P@C nanowires used as an all-in-one electrode. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0089" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0089_R_d140478325e1669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 89 - Chem. Eng. J.">89</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2023, Elsevier. b) Schematic illustration of formation processes for Cu<sub>3</sub>P/TiN and Cu-Fe-P/TiN (TiN stands for Ti net). Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0105" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0105_R_d140478325e1676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 105 - J. Power Sources">105</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2022, Elsevier. c) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of Fe-Ni-P arrays. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0092" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0092_R_d140478325e1682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 92 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">92</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2021, Elsevier. d) Schematic illustration of the process for synthesizing Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>/CN composite. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0091" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0091_R_d140478325e1692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 91 - J. Alloys Compd.">91</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2021, Elsevier. e) Schematic illustration of CoP<sub>2</sub>@C preparation and its sodium ion storage mechanism. Reproduced with permission.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0106" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0106_R_d140478325e1699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 106 - Electrochim. Acta">106</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Copyright 2022, Elsevier.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310426-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310426-sec-0070-title">3.4 Phosphorization</h3>
            
            <p>Phosphorization is also a generally effective strategy for synthesizing metal phosphides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0107" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0107_R_d140478325e1713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 107 - Adv. Energy Mater.">107</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0112" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0112_R_d140478325e1713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 112 - Chem. Eng. J.">112</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, the Cu<sub>3</sub>P and Fe<sub>2</sub>P materials (Cu-Fe-P/TiN) obtained by in situ growth of Cu-PBAs on titanium (Ti) networks followed by phosphorization preparation can be used as unbonded anode and conductive anode for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0105" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0105_R_d140478325e1722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 105 - J. Power Sources">105</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis process is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0004">4b</a>. Cu─Fe─P and Ti mesh are tightly bonded to form regular rod-like particles, forming a conductive network with excellent electrochemical properties. Some researchers added 2&nbsp;mmol Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, 1&nbsp;mmol Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, 6&nbsp;mmol NH<sub>4</sub>F, and 15&nbsp;mmol CO(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub> into 70&nbsp;mL of deionized water and stirred them for 30 min.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0092" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0092_R_d140478325e1751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 92 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">92</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The prepared Ni foam was added to the solution. It was transferred to an autoclave for hydrothermal reaction at 120&nbsp;°C for 4h. Finally, the sample was cleaned with deionized water and dried for 24&nbsp;h to obtain the precursor. After drying, the Fe-Ni-P precursor was placed downstream and 9.4&nbsp;mmol NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O was placed upstream. Fe-Ni-P nanosheet array was obtained by phosphorization in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at 350&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h. The simple synthesis process of Fe-Ni-P framework is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0004">4c</a>. The nanosheet array structure can significantly release mechanical strain and promote electrochemical reaction kinetics. In addition, the synergistic effect of Fe<sub>2</sub>P and Ni<sub>2</sub>P can alleviate volume expansion to a large extent. In addition, the inherent stability of the Fe-Ni-P array frame is a key factor in ensuring that it performs well when cycling. The materials chosen and the array frame designed to exhibit high durability and reliable performance during repeated charge-discharge cycles make it an attractive choice for SIBs. Due to the unique structural design, Fe-Ni-P array can show remarkable Na storage capability. Zeng et&nbsp;al. proposed an efficient three-step method involving “co-precipitation, reduction, and phosphorization” for the in situ synthesis of nano-Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>. Subsequently, it was embedded within a nitrogen-carbon matrix to construct Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>/CN composite material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0091" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0091_R_d140478325e1781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 91 - J. Alloys Compd.">91</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis process is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0004">4d</a>. Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles with a diameter of less than 50&nbsp;nm were successfully prepared using a nitrogen-carbon matrix. Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> is used as the high-capacity core material. Carbon matrix is used as the electronic high-speed channel and mechanical carrier. Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub>/CN constructed in situ has good controllable morphology and can be used as SIBs anode materials, providing a cost-effective strategy for large-scale preparation of high-performance Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> anode and other metal phosphide materials. Hu et&nbsp;al. synthesized phosphate-rich rod-like cobalt phosphide (CoP<sub>2</sub>@C) with carbon shell in the sodium hypophosphate environment by thermal decomposition of polydopamine coated with cobalt oxalate and phosphorization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0106" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0106_R_d140478325e1809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 106 - Electrochim. Acta">106</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis process is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310426-fig-0004">4e</a>. In this core-shell structure, there is a strong interfacial interaction between the CoP<sub>2</sub> core and the carbon shell. The electrical conductivity of the carbon-coated sample is improved, thus providing the possibility to enhance the reaction kinetics and rate capacity. Using Sn<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub>/NG as the precursor, Kong et&nbsp;al. designed and synthesized Sn<sub><i>x</i></sub>P<sub>y</sub>/NG through different phosphorization combined with heat treatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0113" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0113_R_d140478325e1831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 113 - J. Alloys Compd.">113</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is a tin-based phosphide composite with different nanostructures such as nanoclusters, nanoparticles, and yolk–shell nanospheres. For Sn<sub><i>x</i></sub>P<sub>y</sub>/NG, the yolk–shell nanostructure and uniformly dispersed phase of Sn<sub>4</sub>P<sub>3</sub> and SnP<sub>0.94</sub> accelerate the diffusion rate of ions and ensure good structural integrity. The material shows excellent sodium storage properties.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310426-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310426-sec-0080-title">4 Metal Phosphide in SIBs</h2>
         
         <p>Metal phosphides such as Sn─P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0114" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0114_R_d140478325e1858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 114 - Sci. Rep.">114</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe─P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0115" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0115_R_d140478325e1863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 115 - Chem. Commun.">115</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Co─P, and Cu─P have been studied as anode materials for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0116" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0116_R_d140478325e1868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 116 - Nano Energy">116</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0118" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0118_R_d140478325e1868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 118 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">118</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They possess relatively low intercalation potential compared to Na/Na<sup>+</sup> and exhibit higher theoretical gravimetric and volumetric energy density compared to hard carbon, metal oxide, and metal sulfide.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310426-bib-0119" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0119_R_d140478325e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 119 - Adv. Energy Mater.">119</a>-<a href="#smll202310426-bib-0123" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310426-bib-0123_R_d140478325e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 123 - Small">123</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This shows their potential as anodes for high-performance SIBs. The electrochemical properties of SIBs using various metal phosphides as anodes are described in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202310426-tbl-0002">2</a>. However, the capacity of the metal phosphide decays during repeated cycles. This is mainly due to its poor charge transfer kinetics and volume expansion, resulting in powderization and aggregation of the active substance. Many promising approaches have been used to solve these problems. For example, metal phosphides are combined with conductive substrate, or made into core-shell nanostructures and so on.</p>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="smll202310426-tbl-0002">
